Oh you, very dear sons, I have completed this book which is the last of medical knowledge together with its contents, and I have finished it through its explanations and by its declarations. And it came to me in vision that I should complete it with this treatise, which is part of the work of hands, namely "surgery".
And because the work of hands is despised in our region and is looked down upon everywhere, so is the science neglected and lost, and there remains nothing from it except for the little descriptions in old books that the hands have moved. And from this comes the error and vanity, and the intentions are closed and the science is abandoned.
Now it seems to me that I should bring it back to life together with the arrangement of this treatise in the manner of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and I will come together with the forms of cautery instruments and other tools of work with the adjustment of declaration and preparation of them, as is necessary.
And because of this reason that no good masters of hands are found in our time, the art of medicine is abandoned. And it is necessary for the one who undertakes it to ascend in the knowledge of anatomy, as Galen says, so long as he knows the joints of the members, and their forms and conjunctions and separations, and has knowledge of bones and nerves and sinews and the number of them, and the distribution and the moving and resting veins, and from where they issue.
And because of this, Hippocrates says that a name with the name is much, and with work is little; and similarly in the art of hand.
Now we say in the introduction of this book that whoever is not knowledgeable about the things mentioned before, should beware lest he fall into error and harm men; just as I have warned many times those who undertake this science and abandon their knowledge and experience.
For I have seen a foolish surgeon who cut, that is, an abscess of scrofula or neck of a woman, and he cut the arteries of the neck; for what blood flowed out, the woman fell dead in his hands.
And I saw another surgeon who treated a stone in an old man's bladder, and the stone was large; so he took with him a piece of the bladder, and the man died inside three days.
And I had already been called to treat him, and I saw the condition of the patient and the size of the stone, and I knew well that if it were taken out he would die.
And I saw another surgeon who took in charge a duke of our land who had a broken leg, and with the break there was an open wound; and the ignorant surgeon bound the break over the wound with strong linen, and did not leave the wound at all without some kind of ointment or dressing, and left it thus until the end; and he commanded that it should not be untied, and so it remained until the leg became entirely gangrened and the foot came to destruction.
I was called to come to him, and I hastened to come to untie the leg; and when I loosened it, he felt at ease and did not feel any pain.
But despite everything, the corruption had already been confirmed in the member, and I could not heal it; and the corruption did not cease to progress through the member until the entire member was lost.
And I saw another surgeon who opened a suppurating abscess that had become infected, and then it inflamed because of the wickedness of his treatment.
When a carbuncle, insofar as it is pure of melancholic humour, does not need to be touched with iron unless it is in the place where everything that is corrupted can be entirely removed.
And therefore, dear son, you should know that the work of hands is divided into two divisions: that is to say, work where there is danger in many dispositions; and I will show in each place of this book where there is fear and danger.
Why you should guard yourselves, so that foolish people cannot speak ill of you.
If you take your arms in custody, and undertake your illnesses lightly and firmly, and use the best way so that through God's mercy you come to salvation and be praised; and leave frightening diseases to cause grievous sickness; and withdraw your arms from what makes you doubt, and have faith within yourselves so that you may come from this world to the other of glory.
Galien says in some of his admonitions: "Do not take care for a bad illness, unless you are called a foolish doctor."
I have divided this treatise into three chapters.
The first chapter is about cauterizations with fire and medical cauters, and it is the chapter ordered from head to foot; and about the forms of instruments, and the handles of a cauterizer, and all that which is necessary in this work.
The second chapter is about cutting and piercing, and bloodletting, and wounds, and the extraction of splinters, and similar things; and it is a chapter ordered in terms of forms of instruments.
The third chapter is about restoring dislocations and sprains, and similar things; and it is ordered from head to foot, and in terms of forms of instruments.
So that we may remember the work, it is necessary for me to say the quality of help and harm in each temperament.
I say that the qualities of the remedy and its harm are the longest word and subtlest science and secret response; and many wise men spoke about this and agreed with it; and I am satisfied that their words were few because I fear the length.
I say that universal remedy helps every condition where there is matter, except for two conditions: namely hot complexion with matter, and dry complexion with matter.
In hot and dry complexion with matter, they disagreed and said that cautery is great help; and the others said the opposite: that cautery is of no use in diseases that come from heat and dryness.
For the nature of fire is hot, and it should not cure a hot disease with heat or dryness; and those who said the opposite say that cautery makes great help in hot and dry diseases that come into the body of men. For when you compare the body of man and its moisture to the nature of fire, you find the body cold.
And I agree with this word. For I have found it to be true at some time by experience.
And anyway, one should not meddle with it except by someone who has tried it, and who knows through long practice the book of cauteries, and the diversity of human complexions, and the dispositions and causes of their diseases, and the cures and the speed of the remedy; as for other complexions, we have no fear of them, especially in cold and moist diseases. And all the wise men gathered together and agreed on cautery.
And know, son, that cautery with fire is among the secrets of cures, and of greater goodness than burning medicine.
For fire is simple, and comes only to the member that is cooked, and harms no other member if its name be known.
Burning medicines come from member to member spreading disease and bring grievous illness to the afflicted member; and perhaps even kill the patient.
Fire for its intensity and due to the goodness of its substance does not cause such damage, if there is no excess.
And I have found these things through experiment and the long service and study that I have put into this art, and it is based on the truth of things; and for that reason I excuse myself from the length of my speech, because I do not wish to take away from you the secret which is contained in fire, and the quality of the work, how it casts out diseases of the body, just as the philosophers say in demonstrating; so receive them in your thoughts.
And know, son, that they agreed on when the cauterizations should be done and chose attendants.
And I say that cauterization is convenient in all times, because the annoyances which come suddenly are put beneath by the help of the cauterization; and indeed if the cauterizations are for necessary and urgent pains that cannot wait for the time when greater evil comes to the member.
And never think, dear son, as foolish people do; for the disease that is healed by cauterization has no relapse; and they say so themselves. But it's not so as they believe, because cauterizations are only medicines that alter the complexion, and dry up the humors which cause the pain, and are more valuable than other medicines in their haste, virtue, and dominion of their work.
And it is possible that the disease may come again according to the complexion of the patient and the place where the disease was located and its power; and the superfluities are gathered in him through the consumption of foods and similar things, unless cauterizations are administered in the place where there is a lot of superfluity and moisture, such as denser cauterizations for pain, and the like things. And that kind of pain does not return, but this [other type] does happen sometimes.
And many people do not understand why cauterizations are called the last resort of medicine, and it is true; but they do not believe that cauterizations are worth after medicine has been tried, but their intentions are false. And cauterizations are the last resort of medicine because when we administer other kinds of cures in some disease and they do not work, then we administer cauterization, and it works. Then it is clearly understood that cauterization is the last resort of medicine, not according to the intention of foolish physicians.
The ancients said that cauterizers made of gold were more poisonous than those made of iron, and they only said this because of the quality of gold and its high substance; and they said that the wounds caused by cauterization did not become putrid, but this is not true for I have tested it, and I have found that it is true in some people; and gold cauterizers are worth less than iron ones, as they themselves said.
And nonetheless when you heat an instrument of gold, you cannot tell whether it is hot by the color of the gold, and it quickly cools down; and if you heat it too much, it melts in the fire; and thus there are many obstacles.
And because of this we use iron cauterizers that we have made more attractive to our work.
Now I have ordained this chapter from the beginning to end so that the view may be more easily found.

And the old man said that when the wound stays open longer, it purges more; and therefore it brings relief.
And they said that one should cauterize so long that part of the bone is burned, and then remove what has been burned from the bone, and it will be cured.
And the other one said that one should cauterize so long and so strongly that it burns down to the bone, just as wide as a thimble that women use in their sewing, so that the humors may be drained out; and one should leave the wound open for a long time; after that, one should cure and close it.
And I think that both of these methods of cauterization are not entirely good for everyone, except in cases where there is a risk of death. And it seems to me that I should leave them alone; because the skin is weakened by the constant solutions of nature, as we see in other parts of the body; and similarly, when the patient's skin is weak by nature, and the first appearance of the cauterization is more salve and gentle for me, that's what I will follow from now on in order to ensure salvation.

of an aposteme (ulcer) on the throat
When an aposteme (ulcer) comes to the root of the throat or on the uvula, or on the palate, or at the roots of the teeth, and it has made putrefaction and boils and becomes a fistula; and you treat it as we have said in sections, and it does not benefit: then it is necessary that you have a cauterizing iron according to the size of the fistula. from the pain of the teeth
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth ache, and you treat it with medicines and it does not help, then put the head of the sick person on your healthy one. Afterwards heat a cauterizing iron in this form: place the iron over the tooth like a cat on a mouse; and put the hot iron inside hastily, and hold your hand so that the patient feels the heat coming to the root of the tooth; afterwards lift your hand and repeat the process according to your will. Afterwards fill the sick person's mouth with salty water, and let them rinse for an hour and a half.
And in this way the tooth that was aching is made firm, and the softened gums are restrained, and the corrupt moisture is dried up.
of the pain of the teeth
When the pain of the teeth comes from cold or there is an abscess, and the treatments with medicine do not help, then it is necessary to cauterize in two ways: with a poultice (cautere de burre) and with fire.
Cautere de burre is done in this way: take butter from a cow and melt it on the end of an iron or copper spoon; then take some cotton and wrap it around the tip of a tent, and soak it in the melted butter, and place it hastily over the aching tooth and hold until it cools down; and do this often enough until its virtues reach the root of the tooth. And if you see that the wool and cotton are soaked in cold butter, then place them on the aching tooth, and place a hot iron next to it, so that the heat reaches the crown of the tooth.
The cauterization with fire is done in this way: take a cat of iron or copper and make it thick so that the flame does not reach the mouth of the sick person. Then heat the cauterizer, and when its form comes out, place it on the tooth and hold your hand until the cauterizer cools down; do this often enough, because the pain will be gone that same day or the next. And it is necessary for the patient to fill their mouth with good butter in the excitement of that cauterization, and then rinse with cold water; and this is the form of the cauterizer.

The chapters of abscesses
Many kinds of abscesses are described in this book, following the memory that comes later from one and the other, and they vary in perforation and size; and there are two ways in which one is itself similar, and because it holds moisture. And the second way is where the location of the abscess is at the body part. For abscesses of the head are different than those of the foundation, and abscesses of the fatty part are different than those that occur in joints, and each has its own judgment in the work.
And if there are abscesses that should not be pierced until the swelling is dead according to its completion; and those which are so large that they need to be pierced when they are ripe, and do not die according to their completion, such as an abscess near joints. For when an abscess comes near a joint, and you let it alone as long as it swells around it, perhaps the ligaments and nerves of the joint are corrupted by which it causes contraction in that member. Or if the abscess is near the main limb, and you delay piercing it until it is dead, it will harm the main limb; or it is near the foundation, so if you pierce it as soon as it is dead; and you should not pierce a ripe abscess without digestion being completed, except for that which is near the foundation, lest it bursts inward and becomes enlarged, and turns into a fistula; and cannot heal in a long time.
And you should know the time for piercing an abscess that is dead according to its completion, and that is when the pain has subsided, and the fever has broken, and the reduction of pain and redness, and the decrease in swelling and hardness of the head of the abscess, and other signs. of the navel (i.e., the umbilicus)
There are many kinds of navels, whose likenesses I will say.
and now it is fitting that I tell the whole difference which is between navel and umbilicus (issue).
I say that an umbilicus (issue) is hot and feverish, as long as the bubbling is not passed, and the corruption is completed; then the fever and heat subside.
Unlike an umbilicus (issue), a navel has neither heat nor fever nor pain, and contains one sac, which is its own pouch and is of the same color as the body, and begins like jet (a black gemstone) and becomes large.
and they are of two likenesses, either fatty or moist, and have many colors as I said in the description.
It is fitting, when you come to treat the navel, that you first investigate and examine it thoroughly with all the instruments which are called "entrails", according to its shape, whether it comes out as a cord or a scroll, which comes afterwards.
And the manner of examining the swellings in the navel is that you take or make the most moist part of the swelling, and turn your two fingers back and forth, as much as you know the instruments will penetrate the core, then according to the size of the swelling.
then draw out the entrails, and be careful in what comes out afterwards.
If the moist contents are cut, whatever color they have, then treat them as I said for other swellings.
and if afterwards the entrails do not come out moist, know that it is fatty, then treat it in a cross shape, and take it to the forceps, and peel it lightly from each part, and watch where it settles so that you can dress it saine with the navel.
and if you cannot treat it saine, then treat it in pieces as much as possible so that nothing remains.

And know that broken bones are diverse in many ways.

For there are breaks that occur at the angle, and others in splintering, such as the break of a quill pen for writing. And there is a break with an open wound, and a break with a small slit throughout.

Know that the broken members are diverse in smallness and largeness and in form.
For there are small members, such as arms and legs and similar ones: it is necessary to wrap them in fine and subtle linen cloth.
and those that are large, such as thighs and legs: it is necessary to wrap them in a wide and sturdy linen cloth, for the large cloths enclose the entire large member and straighten it equally from all sides, so that there is no crease.

and behold here the manner of ancient plasters to restore breaks, dislocations, and torsions: take flour that is kept near the walls of the mill, and mix it with abundance of egg, and make this concoction neither too clear nor too thick, and use it. And this is the common way of plaster restoring a break in many ways, and especially for women and small children, because it's not too hot or too cold.
Another manner of plaster restoring the break, dislocation, and torsion: take 3 parts of asphalt, 1 part of ladanum (a type of resin), 1 part of naphtha, 1 part of gum arabic, 10 parts of musk, 5 parts of myrrh, 14 parts of aloes, 8 parts of alchemil (a type of root), and grind them into powder. Then mix them with abundance of egg. If the complexion of the person is hot, use this plaster, for it is of good composition and restores broken bones quickly, and is worth many men.
Another manner of plaster that is worth for torsion: take musk and vaseline, 10 parts; myrrh and aloes, 9 parts; aconite, 6 parts; Armenian balm, 10 parts, and grind them well. Then mix with vinegar or abundance of egg, and administer it.
Another manner of plaster for joints and broken bones that are out of their place, and it relieves the pain accompanying them, and is used to cut off the use of the bone and break: take new linen, moisten it in olive oil and let it cool, and put it on the spot. This plaster does not heal, but it is good for soothing hot swelling and properly removing pains.
Another manner of plaster that heals broken bones: take dried leaves of fig and earth leaves, grind them together, and make a plaster.
Another manner of plaster for the restoration of bone break and to reduce the remaining swelling: take roots of camomile, chamomile, flowers of violets, flour of each one part; and grind them, then mix with wine. And if the member is warm, mix it with moist coriander water or vinegar, and use it.
Another manner of plaster that is stronger than the first one, when hard swelling comes to restore the member: take roots of camomile, and seeds of flax, balsam, melilotus, and sanicle, and flowers of violets and chamomile, all in equal parts; and grind them, then mix with salt water or sweet water or with wine, according to the warmth of the member and its rest.
Hippocrates says in his book that for a broken member to be restored well, one should not put anything on it except cerate made of wax and oil, and nothing else, and he says that it should be between two [i.e., neither too soft nor too hard].
And Galen seems to agree that for a broken member to be restored, one should put on it dry things a little warm, such as myrrh and aloes and incense and similar things, just as we have said.
Another manner of plaster that is worth for lesion and pain: take musk and hairs from depilated men or feathers from birds, and equal amounts, and grind them, and make a plaster and apply it.
And then loosen the plaster a little after, and be careful if the patient feels any pain or if the bone has not returned to its place, do not remove the plaster for several days.
And if pain comes to the place or itching, redness, or swelling, hasten and remove it, and take away the plaster now; and take light cloth and a moist sponge of the sea, and plunge them in water, and lift up the place until the itching ceases and the pain is soothed. then let the member rest for an hour, then put on dry linen that has been moistened in water or rose oil, and tie it up with a knot, until you are sure there is no hot swelling.
and let the inflammation of the member rest, and may the pain be removed, then start again with a subtle stitch and a little plaster, and do not bind it too tightly as before, and make them fine until it is healed.
And if you see that the hot swelling and redness and pain and inflammation have completely disappeared, and you need to use plaster and bandages again, then repeat it, just as you did the first time.
And if none of what we have said happens to the member, then do not remove it for three days or four or five or seven; and sometimes let it be twenty days, according to what is seen, until the break is set and approaches the joining of the flesh together.
then the constriction is more than before, and the flesh thickens, as I said before. And if you see that the place dries out more than is necessary, know that putrefaction will not come to it. then wash the place with hot water daily, and each time you remove it, and let it dry a little.
by this work the corruption is cut off from the member, and it heals quickly, unlike what foolish men do, who restore the broken member wrongly: that's bad, and it's a cowardly act, and I see nothing but shame in doing it incorrectly.
Les différentes manières de brisure qui ont été décrites sont : There are many ways of breaking, and various forms, and their causes are destined;
for there are breaks that are caused by the percussion of a sword, and the elbow breaks all the bone just up to the splinter which is under the bone, as done with an axe or a club; and because of this it is called this manner of break "haiche".
or the sword strikes the bone above without piercing it, and is called this manner of break "absolute"; and the wounds of these two breaks are great or small.
And if there is a crushing blow, or splitting, or from the fist of stone, or falling on stone, or similar things.
And if there is a bursting break, which approaches just to the cloth that is under the bone, or it is on the bone, and this wound is either great or small.
And if there is a wound that is closed in the bone as subtle as a thread, and it is the small splitting, and because of this it is called this break "capillary";
and if the wound that is made by being struck with a stone, or by falling, or by a similar thing, and causes the piece of bone to go inwards, and it is the hole that is charred like when blacksmiths heat up their forges; if the stone goes inside.
And this happens more often in those who have soft bones in the skull, such as the skulls of infants.
and this happens with all these kinds of breaks where small pieces are pulled out, just like that; and I will speak about it in its place.
And we know all the kinds of breaks by their signs above them, and by the examination that has been done, and by the foul matter that is on them.
The capillary sign is known by this: when the bone has been uncovered and one rubs it in with their fingers, if the break opens up black.
In the treatment of the break, take care of the bad symptoms of the patient, and if you see no evil accident, such as vomiting blood, loss of understanding, swelling, paleness, fever, or their pulse to be fast and red, and other similar signs in different conditions, then it cannot be healed with any doubt.
And if you see no frightening accident and you hope for salvation, then begin the treatment.
And when the wound comes to you first and it's in winter, then it is convenient to take out the bone before the fourteenth day of the moon that you see.
And if it's in summer, then hasten to take out the bone before the seventh day, so that the flesh under the bone is not corrupted and the accidents that we have said come.
And if the break of the bone comes just to the flesh that covers the brain, then it is necessary for you to split the broken part thus: and so take hold of the patient's head wounded, and uncover on the bone in all the ways that you can, and on the figure of the wound as soft as you can.
and if the bleeding of blood comes out to uncover the bone, or an abscess hot, then take care with what is necessary, and so fill in the place with moistened cloth steeped in rose oil. and make the wound rest as long as the hot abscess is calmed down and you are sure that the bleeding has stopped; then begin around the bone to remove it, and this is done in two ways of work: one way is that you cut with a narrow chisel in front, and here's the shape:
And then I insert another chisel larger than the first one.
And the second way is that you have a variety of chisels, some narrow and others wide, and they are pointed, and made of hard iron; and work lightly, so that you don't harm the head.
And if the bone is hard and dense, then it is necessary that you pierce around it, so that you can insert chisels with handles, but not too deeply that they perforate through to the test of the skull, and on top of the point should be a margin or a circle, that doesn't let the point pass beyond the bone; and you must have several, so that each one goes in its place of the depth of the bone, so that you have handles for all sides; and this is the shape of the three ways of handles:
The quality for piercing around the broken bone is that you place one handle on top of the bone and turn it back and forth until you know the bone is pierced; then move the handle to another place a little later, and do all around in this way; then cut with the nippers the space of the bone that is between the piers, and do so more subtly than you can, and lightly, as we have said, until you bring out the bone into your hand, or onto any of the tools that you have such as tongs, forceps; and be careful not to touch the brain or the spine.
And when you have pierced and removed the bone, then it is necessary that you resaw the remainder of the bone to all one another instrument similar to a nippers, but it should be more sharp and more subtle than the other nippers.
And if any small bones or scales remain, then remove them lightly with one of the instruments; and then treat the wound with tents and ointments as we have said.

if it is adhered to the bone, then chip it lightly, because one chips well without harm and without fear.

Here begins the book of Dislocations.
Dislocation is caused by a joint moving out of its place, so that it cannot move, and the limb is misaligned, causing the patient great pain and great injury.
When dislocation occurs to any joint, it should now be reset to its original place.
Because if you wait, the joint becomes swollen, and by chance if it is reset in pain, then you should not move it to its original place until it has ceased to be swollen: many times it comes to have spasms and other pains.
Anyway, when this happens, you should make the patient rest, then let him be until the swelling has gone down; then warm up the limb with hot water and oil, then gently rub it clean, and cure each member as the doctor will come to its place.
And I have ordered the chapters on dislocation from the head down to the feet.
of the jaws
Something happens when the jaws are dislocated and they hang down, and the dislocation of both is in two ways: either they are a little pulled out of their place and become a little soft; or they are completely dislocated and hang towards the chest, and the saliva from the mouth drips that he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and wrap his tongue with words.
When the dislocation is small, it often returns to its proper place by itself in a short while.
And if it is complete and fully dislocated, you should gently rub it, and not delay.
and in this way that the minister will hold the patient's head and insert the mirror with a small pouch from one of his hands into the root of the jaw dislocation inside the mouth, if it is on both sides; and with the other two hands be outside to put everything back in its place; and order the patient to open and close his jaws and move them all around.
And the mirror is placed in its correct position until it returns to its proper place.
And if it is not easy to return, that is, if it are the two jaws together, then warm them up with hot water and oil, until they can be gently rubbed in; do not delay the return, as we said.
And when they are returned to their proper position and the patient's mouth closes well without swelling, then put on moist cloths soaked in rose oil and wax, and tie them gently with soft strings.
and the patient sleeps on his back and his legs are between two cushions, so that he does not move them to the right or left, nor does he try to move, but rather takes soft food until the pains are gone and the jaws are numb.
and then he should eat lightly what he wants and not force himself to open his mouth while eating or drinking, as long as the jaws are numb and sound.
And if the two jaws cannot be restored to their proper place within an hour and are not returned to their place, it leads to fever and sharp pain, and perhaps colic and maybe vomiting of black bile.
And when you see these things, then know that it is a disaster; and often, in eight days, death comes to the person who has it.

The dislocation of the jaw cannot be released from its original position inside the face and behind the earlobe, and is released only once on the outside, and one can feel it well.
And the restoration is that the patient lies on his back and stretches out his arms; then he presses firmly on the dislocated point with your palm, and it will return to its place; then put on a poultice and bandage it in place.

and similarly is returned the leg of the hip when it is dislocated from its place.
Of the dislocation of the shoulder
It is known that the shoulder is dislocated in three ways: one is when it dislocates at the lower part of the arm bone; and the second is at the upper part of the shoulder blade; and perhaps it dislocates at the top, and this rarely happens.
and it does not dislocate backwards for the shoulder, and it does not dislocate inward for the nerves; and it dislocates downward toward the arm bone many times, especially in those who have little flesh, for then it slips out quickly; in those who have enough flesh, it jumps out slowly, and comes back slowly.
And perhaps it happens to some men that there is a blow or crushing, and the shoulder swells with a hot tumor, and they think it has been dislocated because of this.
You must then examine it until you are certain; and then begin the treatment.

and similarly, if it is dislocated upwards, it is not hidden, because you find it clearly at the shoulder.
and this manner of dislocation is not to be reduced when it is new or if the child has an illness.

of the elbow joint.

and when the doctor has joined the joint to his hand, he sprinkles some oil on it to loosen the joint, then pushes the joint firmly, until it comes back.
and when it is back in place, he puts on dry plaster with plenty of egg, and wraps the arm firmly, and lets the patient's arm rest for a while, then releases it.
And if the joint is strong and in its place, he releases the bandage and lets it be; and if you see that the joint does not heal well, he repeats the plaster and the bandage, and lets it rest for a while, until it is firmly set, then he releases it.
And if he feels great pain after the reduction of the joint, and it becomes inflamed, he bathes and rubs it gently, and presses until it is relieved; or he puts a thick sheep's wool poultice on the joint, then lets it be for one day and one night, then removes it and makes him enter the bath. And when he sweats, he rubs the joint gently, then puts the poultice back on, and then makes him enter the bath; and thus does it two or three times until he is healed; and if you see that, he puts on warm, moistened linseed flax with butter, and wraps it around; it will return to its normal state.

Dislocation of the wrist (or forearm). It seems like we've been reading a medieval medical text on how to treat this condition!
At times the forearm is dislocated in either direction, sometimes towards the back of the palm or into the belly.
Then the forearm will be in its place, and push the dislocation back into its socket as far as it returns. Then support the head of the forearm, and move it towards the part where it was disjointed.
and let it be for 2 days, and then put it back in its place that same day. And from then on bind it as we have said, and release the bandage every night, and make it move, and rebind it tightly; and do this often, until it becomes strong again.
and do exactly the same thing if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand, and do so just as you have done here before, until it is healed; and do exactly the same thing when it is dislocated in any other way.
The dislocation of the spine (vertebrae) of the back. Another potentially serious medical condition!
When the vertebra of the back or of the neck is dislocated, or if there are more than one that are dislocated, there is no cure, for the bone quickly kills the patient.
And the signs are that the excretions jump out without any will, and he cannot hold them, and many times they are loosened in some member, or both thighs, or the two arms, or one.
And many times the vertebrae move in four parts; that which moves at the back is called "gyphosis".
the cure is that you reward him if the gyphosis came from childhood: then there is no cure or healing at all.
And that which comes from falling or blows or similar things, the old-fashioned methods of cures were to use great words, and it was not a great benefit in all cases.
but I have shortened it according to my intention, and I have made instruments in various ways.
I say that in the kyphosis (or hunchback) that comes at the front of the chest, there is no cure nor healing.
and also that which comes straight to the sides, and one cannot cure it except for that which properly comes to the back, as I say;
and it is that the patient should be stretched out on his back in one position equally, and have a poultice (or strain) under him so that he does not get hurt.
then place a heated iron at his head on the ground firmly, and another at his feet, and bind a strip of cloth over the joints of the patient, and one to his hips, legs, knees, and ankles.
and two attendants know how to bind the strips of cloth, one at the head and the other at the feet, between the two heated irons.
and let them be well closed, the iron as we have said, and the attendant places his hands on the patient's spine firmly, until it returns to its normal position.
And if it does not return with this treatment, then take a board of three arms length in width, and sharpen the edge on one side, and have the patient lie on their side, and place the head of the board on the back's skin, then put the middle part of the board on the spine. and have the attendant place their feet on the other end of the board, and press well, until it returns to its proper position; and if you see fit, do the same treatment to the vertebra that turns towards the hand.

and then bind the bandage that is between the joints to the turning iron near the head, and the other bandage to the other turning iron towards the feet; and each of the attendants turns their turning iron with all the bandage, and the master puts the spine straight as we have said.

The Dislocations of the Hip
It is known that the joints of the hip and the joints of the shoulder are prone to dislocations alone, and it is not like other joints; and the joint of the hip is displaced in four parts: inside and outside, and front and back; and more inwardly towards the contrary side, and somewhat displaced in front and back.
And the knowledge of its dislocation inside is that when one joins the sick leg with the healthy one, it becomes longer, and the sick knee joint opens more than the healthy one, and the sick person cannot bend their knee in front. and it is the place where there are swellings of hot abscess, because the tendons of the thigh come to that place.
And the sign of the one whose dislocation is in the back is that this accident is contrary to those in the front.
And the signs of the one whose dislocation is in the front are that they bend their leg well according to its completion; however, they do not double their knee with pain, and if one wants them to walk, they cannot move forward, and retain their urine, and swell the ankle, and when they go, it passes over the heel.
And the signs of the one whose dislocation is in the back are that they cannot straighten their knee, nor bend it, and that leg is more twisted than the other, and the ankle is softened, and the tendons of the thigh are at the place under the ribs.
The remedies for this condition of dislocation are that you return to see if the dislocation is ancient, and it remains in its position, then there is no cure; and one must not have any presumption.
And in the one whose dislocation is fresh, and it is of three manners of dislocation, then hurry to turn the joint inward and outward, and move it from right to left; perhaps it will return, and there will be no need for another cure.
And if it does not return in this manner, then you must have a strong assistant appear, and stretch the leg strongly with two hands, or with one bandage that is bound over the knee. and another attendant stretches their hand up in such a way that they press their hands under the patient's buttocks, then tightens it with a strong bandage over the thigh; and another one ties the end of the bandage.
and stretch it upwards and downwards all at once, until the patient is lifted off the ground for an instant, and remains suspended.
This method of stretching is common to all three appearances, and if the dislocation has returned, as we have said, it is well.
If it is not so, then you must apply a cure to its appearance. When the dislocation is in the part behind, then you must bind the patient on their healthy side, then place the bandage over the root of the thigh, and the place under the ankle, then stretch the bandage upwards towards the ankle, and towards the muscle.
then take another attendant's arms and encircle the thick part of the sick thigh, and stretch it out to the back firmly: it will return to its place now; and this manner of dislocation is lighter than the others.
And if you cannot bind it in this manner that we have said, then you must tie the two feet of the patient together with a strong bandage soft on the two heels and on the two knees, and let the sick leg be stretched two fingers longer than the other.
then let it be hung up on the head of a beam, and let it be suspended above the ground two arms' length; then command one attendant to encircle the head of the thigh, and have another attendant hang with the patient, who encircles firmly the thigh, because the joint returns now hastily.
And when the dislocation is in the back, then let the patient lie on their side, as we have said in the one with kyphosis, and stretch a bandage over the sick leg properly, and over the pubic bone as well.
then put two irons, one at the head and one at the feet, then insert one iron between the thighs of the patient in the middle. and have damp cloths wrapped around it so that the patient is not hurt, then have each attendant stretch towards them and the physician puts their hand on the dislocation to guide them.
If it returns, that's good. If not, put them on the table and press them as we have said in kyphosis, and let the patient lie on their healthy side.
And when the dislocation is in front, it is necessary to stretch the sick leg hastily and as I have said; and the physician should place their hand over the painful ankle, then press with the other hand and stretch downwards towards the knee.
And if the dislocation is at the back, it is not necessary to stretch the patient downwards or lift them up onto their feet, but rather they should be positioned as if the hip were displaced backwards, as we have said in the bed with two hips. and be on their back, and the bandage stretched as we have said before, and one must press on the joint at the table.
and it will be in place when the patient stretches out their legs and they are equal; then align the thighs, apply a poultice, and bind with a bandage so that the hip does not move.
and the patient rests for three or four days, then you remove the bandage and apply a poultice on top of it; and if you see that they are equal, then you bind them as before, and leave it so for three days, then remove it and let the patient walk slowly until the limbs are comfortable.
The Dislocation of the Knee
The knee joint is displaced in three ways: that is to say, inward and outward, and backward; and it does not get displaced forward.
And the signs are that you command the patient to bend their leg to their thigh, and if it does not straighten out to its normal position, know that it is displaced.
and the restoration of all this is that you have the patient sit up straight, and stretch out their leg if they can, and place your back on their chest, and put their foot on your feet.
then take their knee in your hands and pull it back between your fingers, then bend the two sides of the knee joint firmly; and have another attendant stretch out their foot until the knee joint returns to its place, and the sign is that they straighten out their leg easily.
and apply the poultice on top of it, and have them stretch out their leg to their thigh, and bind them together firmly for three or four days, then release them; and do not let the patient walk again until the limbs are comfortable.
And if the members are not in place, administer strong stretching, as we have said in the treatment of the hip, until they are healed.
The Dislocation of the Ankle
The ankle is sometimes sprained and sometimes completely dislocated, both inward and outward.
And the signs are that the calf is swollen, and tends to turn towards the part where it is dislocated:
the remedy is light, and one should stretch it lightly with their hands when it is only sprained.
And when it is completely dislocated, then you must have the patient sit up straight and have one attendant stretch out their foot over the ankle from above.
then take the foot in your right hand and then in your left hand twice, then stretch it upwards and press firmly the foot up.
and do this twice, as we have said, pushing and pulling the foot; if it returns, that's good; if not, then start again with the work, because it will return.
and if it cannot return in this way, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground and insert a wooden bar firmly into the earth between their thighs, and wrap cloths around to prevent them from being hurt.
then have the attendant tie the thigh, and another attendant stretch out the foot to their hands or with one bandage, so that it is tied around the ankle's collar. then stretch both attendants equally, and the bar be placed between the patient's thighs to hold the patient in place when they are being stretched.
then have the physician guide their hands to the dislocation, and another attendant ties the healthy leg below it, and the dislocation will return hastily.
And when it is in place, then apply the poultice and bandages on top, and bind them firmly, and tie the foot to the leg, and watch the nerves that are behind the ankle, so that the bandage is not too tight.
then let it be for two or three days, and if the bandage loosens, bind again; then release it on the third or fourth day, and have the patient rest and not walk for four days. and if he walks before this time is up, it's not certain that he won't dislocate again.
And if hot abscesses come, then you must alleviate them, as we have said elsewhere, until they are healed.
The Wrist
When the wrist is dislocated, it should be stretched carefully without great effort, and it's a light thing.
And if the dislocation is in any part of the back of the foot, then you must have the patient sit on a flat stone or on a table right up straight, and place their foot on what is visible of the joint, then press with their own foot firmly until it returns.
and when it is equal and there is nothing visible, then place under the sole of the foot a board with two legs, then stretch firmly and leave for three days, then release; and have the patient rest until they are healed.
Wounds and Dislocations Together
When a wound comes with a dislocation, and one wishes to restore it, many times there will be that the patient dies.
If you are not presumptuous in the treatment, if you are not wise in the art and have not used it for a long time, and if you are not merciful and slow to act, nor too hasty;
and use soothing remedies first, a warm poultice, and leave the patient in God's hand if you see no hope of recovery. and when you see hope, then remove the dislocation now so that the warm poultice comes to the place.
and when the limb is in place, administer soothing remedies and warm poultices, and cure the wound if it seems fitting, with drying ointments.
and if there is a break with the dislocation and small bones are shattered, then set them, and do what we have said in simple diseases.
and beware of entering the path of fear, as I have said, for this will harm you according to your Creator, and for the knowledge of your arm and body's safety. Amen.
